### [Why Uttarakhand forests are on fire this season](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/why-uttarakhand-forests-are-on-fire-this-season-13768602.html)

The article discusses the severe forest fires in Uttarakhand, which have claimed four lives and affected over 1,318 hectares of land. Local communities, including social worker Vijay Jardari, are actively fighting the fires with makeshift tools. The fires have caused significant air quality and visibility issues, prompting a large-scale response involving forest staff, locals, and even the Indian Air Force.

Chief Minister Pushkar Dhami has emphasized the government's efforts to control the fires and punish those responsible for starting them. The article highlights that most forest fires are man-made, often due to low rainfall and dry conditions. Villagers sometimes start fires to clear land or as a precaution against approaching fires.

The local community's role is crucial in early fire intervention, as forest department staff may take time to arrive. Environmentalist Chandi Prasad Bhatt stresses the need for village groups and support to combat forest fires.

This season's fires have resulted in high casualties, with four deaths and several injuries. No animal casualties have been reported. The destruction includes over 16 hectares of plantation and 427 trees. Pine needles, which contribute to fire spread, are suggested to be used for power generation and other products.

Rainfall has helped control fires in some districts, and more is predicted, offering hope for the situation's improvement. The community is relying on the forecasted rains to help extinguish the fires and restore normalcy.

France seeks to position itself as a bridge between Europe and the Indo-Pacific.

**Challenges in France-US Relations:** The historical alliance between France and the US has seen ups and downs, with disagreements over the Iraq War and the AUKUS agreement. However, cooperation has improved recently, particularly in response to Russia's invasion of Ukraine.

**India-France Relations:** The long-standing relationship between India and France is characterized by trust and cooperation, particularly in defense and strategic interests. The two countries are working closely in the Indo-Pacific region and have committed to deepening their defense industrial partnership. France supports India's bid for permanent membership in the UN Security Council.

**Conclusion:** The article suggests that while China remains a competitor with conflicting interests with France, France views India as a more reliable long-term global partner. The strategic congruence of interests between India and France is seen as beneficial for both countries.

### [How indigenous BrahMos missiles can boost India’s defence export](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/how-indigenous-brahmos-missiles-can-boost-indias-defence-export-13762460.html)

The article discusses the success and future prospects of the BrahMos missile, an Indo-Russian joint venture. The missile, named after the Brahmaputra and Moskva rivers, is expected to achieve full indigenization by 2026. With over 12 countries currently negotiating for its purchase, India anticipates exporting $3 billion worth of BrahMos missiles by 2026. The missile is versatile, with land, sea, submarine, and air versions, and is considered the world's fastest, most accurate, and deadliest supersonic cruise missile.

Russia, holding a near-equal partnership in the BrahMos venture, supports the export to countries friendly to both Russia and India. The BrahMos is strategically significant for India, especially as a deterrent against China in the Indian Ocean and South China Sea. The Philippines has already placed a $375 million order for the land-to-sea version, and other countries like the UAE and Saudi Arabia are potential buyers.

India's defense strategy includes expanding its list of defense attaches and potential BrahMos export countries. The next generation of BrahMos missiles will have increased range and speed, with India poised to become a major Asian armaments exporter. The article concludes with the author's personal views, emphasizing India's strategic advancements and the lack of international interest in Chinese and Pakistani armaments.

Mon, 22 Apr 2024 17:50:54 +0530
### [How extreme weather shapes electoral outcomes](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/how-extreme-weather-shapes-electoral-outcomes-13762392.html)

Extreme weather, like heatwaves, has been studied for its impact on voter turnout and behavior. While it might seem logical that extreme heat would discourage voting, research indicates a more nuanced relationship. Studies show that warmer temperatures can actually correlate with higher voter turnout and support for the incumbent party. In India, where extreme heat is common during elections, temperature spikes above 36°C can increase voter turnout, especially in rural areas where agriculture is affected. This can influence candidate preference, with voters favoring candidates with agricultural backgrounds who they believe can address climate change challenges in farming. As global temperatures rise, the frequency of heatwaves is expected to increase, posing challenges for electoral participation. Considering the impact of extreme heat on voter behavior, scheduling elections during milder months could help ensure a more equitable and comfortable voting process.

It aims to prepare officers for leadership and enhance the Navy's warfighting capabilities while contributing to the nation's intellectual capital.

### [40 years of Operation Meghdoot: Siachen and Indian military's knockout punch to Pakistan](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/40-years-of-operation-meghdoot-siachen-and-indian-militarys-knockout-punch-to-pakistan-13758990.html)

Operation Meghdoot, a pivotal military operation in India's history, was launched in April 1984 to secure the Siachen Glacier region. The dispute over Siachen traces back to the Karachi Agreement of 1949, which delineated the Ceasefire Line but left the glacier undefined due to its inhospitable nature. Pakistan's attempts to stake claim to the area escalated tensions, prompting India to take action.

Led by Colonel Narinder (Bull) Kumar, Indian military expeditions in the late 1970s and early 1980s revealed Pakistan's incursions into the Siachen area. This led to India's decision to establish a presence in the region, supported by the Indian Air Force's logistical assistance.

Operation Meghdoot commenced on April 13, 1984, with the Indian Army seizing key passes to preempt Pakistani aggression. Despite facing harsh weather and Pakistani attacks, Indian forces gradually gained control of strategic positions, including Bilafond La and Quaid Post (later renamed Bana Top).

The Kargil War of 1999 further highlighted the importance of Siachen, as Indian troops captured Pakistani posts in the region. Operation Meghdoot's legacy is characterized by the resilience and valor of Indian soldiers, with joint efforts from the Indian Army and Air Force ensuring India's control over Siachen.

The operation stands as a testament to the dedication of Indian armed forces personnel, with Siachen remaining a symbol of India's military history and sacrifice.

### [Demystified CAA for the common man: It includes and does not exclude](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/demystified-caa-for-the-common-man-it-includes-and-does-not-exclude-13758656.html)

The article discusses the Citizenship Amendment Act (CAA) of 2019 in India, which has been a subject of significant debate and protest. Here's a summary:

- **Background**: The CAA amends the Citizenship Act of 1955 and is part of a shift from birthright citizenship (jus soli) to citizenship by descent (jus sanguinis). It has faced substantial opposition in the Indian political landscape.
  
- **Provisions**: The CAA provides a pathway to Indian citizenship for persecuted minorities from Pakistan, Afghanistan, and Bangladesh who arrived before December 31, 2014. It reduces the waiting period for citizenship from 11 to 5 years for these groups but does not change the process for other migrants.
  
- **Exemptions**: The Act does not apply to existing Indian citizens or to migrants who entered India after 2014. It also excludes certain regions in northeast India.
  
- **Controversies**: Critics argue that the CAA violates the Assam Accord and discriminates against Muslims, thus threatening India's secular ethos. The Act is also under scrutiny for potentially being unconstitutional under Article 14, which guarantees the right to equality.
  
- **Legal Challenges**: The CAA has been challenged in the Supreme Court of India, with the primary contention being its alleged contravention of Article 14. The matter is currently sub-judice.
  
- **Perspective**: The author, an advocate, argues that the CAA was enacted for humanitarian reasons and does not threaten the citizenship of existing Indian citizens. They suggest focusing on India's progress and economic growth rather than getting entangled in debates over the CAA, which is pending judicial review.

The author's views are personal and do not necessarily reflect those of the publication. The article emphasizes that the CAA is not applicable to current Indian citizens and that the protests and legal challenges are based on concerns about its constitutionality and potential discrimination. The situation is evolving, with the Supreme Court's decision pending.

### [India’s Unified Air Traffic Management System: A giant leap towards safety, efficiency](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/indias-unified-air-traffic-management-system-a-giant-leap-towards-safety-efficiency-13758323.html)

India is advancing its civil aviation capabilities with the initiation of the Indian Single Sky Harmonised (ISHAN) air traffic management system. This initiative aims to merge the country's four Flight Information Regions (FIRs) into one unified airspace, allowing for a seamless flow of air traffic and improved safety and efficiency. The ISHAN system will be centrally controlled from Nagpur and is expected to optimize air traffic management, reduce travel time and fuel consumption, and lower the carbon footprint.

In 2023, Indian carriers transported an average of 417,000 passengers daily, with an average of 2,891 domestic flights per day. The Airports Authority of India (AAI) has begun seeking consultants to develop the ISHAN concept and source technologies both domestically and globally. The integration model is inspired by the Indian Air Force's (IAF) Integrated Air Command and Control System (IACCS), which has successfully connected all IAF sensors, offices, and personnel through the secure Air Force Network (AFNet).

The consolidation of FIRs under ISHAN will involve digital connectivity, radar integration, infrastructure development, and controller training. Once implemented, air traffic controllers in Nagpur will handle all domestic flights in upper airspace, reducing the need for coordination among regional controllers and improving flight efficiency. The unified airspace will also provide a combined air picture to all ATCs, aiding in traffic management and emergency response.

The ISHAN initiative is supported by advanced technologies such as ground-based and airborne radars, satellite-based sensors, GPS, and India's NavIC for accurate positioning. The Traffic Alert and Collision Avoidance System (TCAS) and the Terrain Avoidance and Warning System (TAWS) enhance safety, while the Future Air Navigation System (FANS) facilitates direct communication between pilots and ATC.

India's move towards a single airspace reflects its growth as a major civil aviation market, with the number of aircraft expected to reach 1,500 by 2027-28. The integration of civil and military aviation through ISHAN represents a significant step towards enhancing India's Comprehensive National Power (CNP) and air traffic efficiency. The views expressed in the article are personal to the author, the Director General of the Centre for Air Power Studies, and do not necessarily reflect those of Firstpost.

### [Remembering Daniel Kahneman: The architect of behavioral economics](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/remembering-daniel-kahneman-the-architect-of-behavioral-economics-13758179.html)

I'm sorry, but I can't provide verbatim excerpts from copyrighted texts. However, I can offer you a summary of the key points from the article about Professor Daniel Kahneman:

- **Daniel Kahneman**, a renowned psychologist and Nobel laureate, passed away on March 27, 2024, at the age of 90.
- He is celebrated for his groundbreaking work in behavioral economics, challenging traditional microeconomic theories by demonstrating that human decisions are often irrational.
- Kahneman's collaboration with Amos Tversky led to the development of **Prospect Theory**, which shows that people's attitudes toward risk in decision-making are inconsistent.
- His research revealed that the human mind, while efficient in some areas, is prone to errors and biases in others, significantly impacting decision-making.
- Kahneman's book, *Thinking, Fast and Slow*, introduces two systems of thought: **System 1** (fast, intuitive, and often erroneous) and **System 2** (slow, deliberate, and logical).
- He identified various cognitive biases that affect judgment and decision-making, such as confirmation bias, anchoring, and the sunk-cost fallacy.
- Kahneman advocated for awareness of these biases to improve decision-making in individuals and organizations, as supported by a McKinsey study showing improved investment returns when biases were reduced.
- He proposed the **Peak-End Rule**, suggesting that people judge experiences based on their most intense points and their endings.
- Kahneman distinguished between happiness (a transient state) and satisfaction (a long-term feeling based on achieving goals), finding that day-to-day happiness plateaus after reaching an annual income of $75,000.
- Despite his significant contributions, Kahneman remained humble about his legacy, which continues to influence and inspire future generations.

Please note that this summary is based on the content provided and does not include any direct quotations from the article. If you're interested in more detailed insights, I recommend reading Kahneman's works directly.

### [Weather report: Wet weekend ahead for northern states while south India heats up](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/weather-report-wet-weekend-ahead-for-northern-states-while-south-india-heats-up-13757332.html)

Here's a summary of the article:

North India is expected to experience a delay in the onset of core summer conditions for another three weeks due to predicted above-normal rainfall and below-normal temperatures starting April 13. Southern India has already faced a harsh summer, with high temperatures and delayed pre-monsoon rains. The first week of April was particularly dry and hot, with Bengaluru and Nandyal recording their highest temperatures of the season at 37.6°C and 44.5°C, respectively.

Several regions in Andhra Pradesh also reported temperatures above 43°C. Similarly, parts of east and west India experienced temperatures over 40°C from April 3rd to 6th. North India is warming up as well, with temperatures ranging from 34°C to over 40°C in some areas of Rajasthan and Uttar Pradesh.

Rainfall from March 1st to April 7th has been below normal across India, with a 14% deficit compared to the average. The southern peninsula has seen an 82% departure from normal rainfall, while central India has a 67% surplus.

Weather patterns influencing India's climate include a western disturbance over northwest Uttar Pradesh, a trough/wind discontinuity from interior Odisha to north Tamil Nadu, and cyclonic circulations over east Assam and southeast Rajasthan. These conditions are expected to bring rainy afternoons and evenings to central and eastern India from April 8th to 12th, with scattered thunderstorms and a drop in maximum temperatures.

While east India will see wet conditions, the western and southern regions will continue to face heat until April 14th. Pre-monsoon rains are expected to begin in Telangana, Andhra Pradesh, and Kerala from April 11th. Northern states will also have a wet weekend ahead, with stormy weather predicted due to western disturbances and south-west winds from April 13th to 14th, leading to a temporary halt in the rise of summer temperatures.

The article discusses the ambiguity surrounding Dhruvak's commissioning status and its role as a mooring vessel, essential for laying buoys in the era when berthing space was scarce. Despite its auxiliary status, Dhruvak played a vital role and is fondly remembered by naval veterans.

Despite global supply chain challenges, HAL secured new contracts worth over Rs 35,000 crore and achieved a significant milestone in defense exports, surpassing Rs 21,000 crore for the first time.

The company's history dates back to 1940, with its establishment in Bangalore and subsequent nationalization during World War II. Over the years, HAL has evolved into a major aerospace and defense PSU, with 11 R&D centers and 21 manufacturing divisions across India. It operates under the Ministry of Defence and employs nearly 24,500 people.

HAL's financial performance remains strong, with substantial growth in operating and net income, and its shares have seen a remarkable increase. The order book exceeds Rs 94,000 crore, with expectations of more orders in the coming fiscal year.

Key achievements include the delivery of the LCA 'Tejas' Mk 1A to the Indian Air Force (IAF) and international partnerships, such as delivering HAL-built Dorniers to Guyana. HAL's product range spans fighter jets, helicopters, jet engines, and avionics, with significant contributions to both military and civilian sectors.

The company faces challenges in original design and intellectual property investment, with a need to adopt more corporate practices and compete beyond guaranteed Indian armed forces orders. HAL's future plans include setting up logistic bases internationally and focusing on the Make in India policy to boost defense exports.

### [Vantage | What will be domestic and global impact of Katchatheevu controversy?](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/vantage-what-will-be-domestic-and-global-impact-of-katchatheevu-controversy-13755170.html)

The article discusses the Katchatheevu controversy, which has become a focal point in India's upcoming general elections. Here's a summary:

**Background:**
Katchatheevu is an uninhabited island located between India and Sri Lanka, historically disputed by both countries. In 1974, India, under the Congress government, ceded the island to Sri Lanka.

**Current Controversy:**
Prime Minister Narendra Modi has brought attention to this historical decision, suggesting it was careless and compromised India's unity. This has been fueled by an RTI petition filed by Modi's party, bringing the issue back into the spotlight. External Affairs Minister S Jaishankar linked the 1974 deal to the arrest of Indian fishermen by Sri Lanka, implicating the Congress and DMK parties in the decision.

**Domestic Implications:**
The controversy resonates with Tamil voters, especially fishermen, for whom Katchatheevu is part of their livelihood and cultural identity. The BJP is leveraging this sentiment to gain support in Tamil Nadu, where it has historically had limited success.

**International Implications:**
Sri Lanka maintains its sovereignty over Katchatheevu, potentially causing diplomatic tensions with India. The issue also highlights broader challenges in India's border management and the need for robust diplomatic engagement.

**Conclusion:**
The Katchatheevu controversy underscores the complexities of India's foreign policy and the lasting impact of historical decisions on current affairs, emphasizing the importance of territorial disputes in the nation's political discourse.

Please note that the views in the article are the author's personal opinions and do not necessarily reflect those of Firstpost. The content is copyrighted, and I have provided a summary without reproducing any copyrighted material.

### [Commerce, investment and economic expansion: India-EFTA trade agreement](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/commerce-investment-and-economic-expansion-india-efta-trade-agreement-13754529.html)

The article discusses the **Trade and Economic Partnership Agreement** between **India** and the **European Free Trade Association (EFTA)**, which includes Switzerland, Norway, Liechtenstein, and Iceland. Signed on March 10, 2024, after nearly 16 years of negotiations, the agreement promises a $100 billion investment and aims to boost trade and investment by reducing tariffs and relaxing regulations.

Key points of the agreement include:
- **14 chapters** covering trade in goods, services, investment promotion, IPRs, and more.
- **Benefits for India**, such as liberalized trade rules, tariff discounts in the EU, and opportunities for professionals to work across the EFTA region.
- **Concessions from EFTA** in service industries like accounting, auditing, and law.
- **Access for Indian industry** to the EU market through EFTA, including tariff concessions on agricultural products and the elimination of duties on Swiss watches and chocolates over seven years.

The agreement is seen as a significant step towards economic integration, with potential benefits in commerce, investment, and economic growth for both parties. However, concerns remain regarding the implementation and the legal binding nature of the private sector's investment from the EFTA countries. The overall success of the agreement will depend on its execution and the utilization of the opportunities it presents.

The views expressed are personal to the author, a former ICWA research intern, and do not necessarily reflect those of Firstpost.

### [Indelible mark of democracy: The story of India’s election ink](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/indelible-mark-of-democracy-the-story-of-indias-election-ink-13754474.html)

The article discusses the history and chemistry of the indelible ink used in Indian elections. It traces the ink's origins to a patent filed in 1950 by the National Research and Development Corporation and its first use in India's 1962 general election. The ink, produced by Mysore Paints and Varnish Limited, contains silver nitrate, which reacts with the salt on human skin to form silver chloride, turning the ink from purple to black. This reaction was first utilized by George Eastman in photographic film. The article also touches on the political and social implications of the ink, mentioning its use as a deterrent against voter fraud and its symbolic significance. It notes that around 30 countries use electoral ink, mainly in the "third world," and speculates on the future of electoral ink in light of technological advances like biometric identification. The author, an Emeritus Professor, shares personal views, which do not necessarily reflect those of the publication. The article concludes with thoughts on the potential obsolescence of indelible ink as electronic validation methods become more prevalent.

The corridor, which stretches from China's Xinjiang province to Pakistan's Gwadar port, is threatened by security concerns stemming from various militant groups, including Uyghur fighters, the Pakistani Taliban, and the Baluchistan Liberation Army. The economic downturn has led to Pakistan defaulting on payments for Chinese-built infrastructure, and there are calls for renegotiation of loan terms with China and other lenders.

Diplomatic maneuvers are underway, with the US showing interest in the region, potentially as a counterbalance to China's influence. The recent election of a leader from the Gwadar Rights Movement in Pakistan's provincial assembly highlights local discontent with the CPEC's impact on the region.

China, facing its own challenges, is recalibrating its Belt and Road Initiative to focus on smaller projects, moving away from larger investments like the CPEC. The situation remains complex, with no easy solutions in sight for the involved parties.

- TS Murty, an Indian official, received the Dalai Lama and escorted him to safety, treating him as an honored guest.
- This act of refuge had significant repercussions, leading to border clashes with China later that year.

**China's Stance:**
- Despite not protesting the Dalai Lama's entry into India in 1959, China now claims the region as its own, contradicting its earlier stance.
- The article suggests that if China had considered Tawang part of its territory then, it would have pursued the Dalai Lama into India.

- The events of March 1959 in Tibet included a popular uprising and a brutal crackdown by Chinese forces.

### [Traditional solutions to Karnataka’s modern water crisis](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/traditional-solutions-to-karnatakas-modern-water-crisis-13744601.html)

Bangalore faces a severe water crisis, highlighting the urgent need for water conservation. The city's traditional water bodies have dwindled due to urbanization, with Karnataka losing over 13,000 water bodies in just 2021-2022.

Traditional water bodies, such as the massive tanks in Chitradurga Fort and the stepped tank in Santhebennur, demonstrate sustainable water management techniques. These structures use natural gradients to capture and distribute water effectively.

Temple tanks, like the Panchkuta Kalyani in Melukote, emphasize the cultural and religious significance of water bodies. By reviving these traditional water bodies and creating new spaces for water, modern city dwellers can address water scarcity.

Potential homebuyers should consider the history of their property to ensure they are not contributing to the loss of water bodies. The future of cities depends on the sustainable management of water resources, learning from traditional techniques and prioritizing water conservation.

### [Why political leaders like Truman, Bush never choke before taking tragic decisions](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/book-review-famed-cognitive-scientist-sian-beilock-explains-how-human-mind-takes-decisions-13744544.html)

The article explores the concept of "choking" under pressure and its impact on decision-making. It argues that highly skilled individuals can experience performance failures when the stakes are high due to overactivity in the prefrontal cortex, which inhibits automatic skills. The author cites examples of historical events, such as the Iraq invasion and Hiroshima bombing, where choking could have had devastating consequences.

Cognitive scientist Sian Beilock proposes strategies to combat choking, including focusing on the task, developing routines, rehearsing success, and using meditation or mantras. The article also discusses the concept of stereotype threat, where negative stereotypes can impair performance. The author questions why some individuals, like former President Donald Trump, seem immune to choking under pressure, suggesting that future research may shed light on how politicians navigate high-stress situations.

### [New criminal laws: An era of accountability, transparency and justice](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/new-criminal-laws-an-era-of-accountability-transparency-and-justice-13744274.html)

Three new criminal laws (BSS, BNS, BNSS) will replace the IEA, IPC, and CrPC, bringing significant changes:

* Electronic records will be included as "documents" in evidence, simplifying the process for admitting digital evidence.

* Zero FIRs will be mandatory, allowing reports of cognizable offenses regardless of jurisdiction, reducing the need for victims to seek FIR registrations from multiple jurisdictions.

* Search and seizures by police will be video-recorded, promoting transparency and reducing potential misuse of police power.

* Forensic experts will be required to collect forensic evidence in cases with penalties of seven years or more, improving the quality of evidence gathering.

* Victims or complainants will be informed of investigation progress within 90 days.

* Judgments must be delivered within 30 days of completing arguments, ensuring a speedier trial process.

These reforms aim to modernize India's criminal justice system by incorporating technology, promoting transparency, and ensuring accountability throughout the investigation and trial process.

### [The stray dog: Not a stray issue for India anymore](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/the-stray-dog-not-a-stray-issue-for-india-anymore-13743748.html)

India has the highest number of stray dog attacks and rabies deaths worldwide. Stray dog incidents have sparked outrage, leading to calls for culling or selective slaughter. However, the author questions the validity of blaming stray dogs and suggests examining the root cause.

The tragic death of a child in Delhi raises suspicions about the possible involvement of an aggressive Pitbull kept by the family, rather than stray dogs. Illegal breeding of aggressive breeds for dog fights and entertainment has become prevalent, leading to the creation of vicious animals.

While some instances of stray dog misbehavior exist, they also have heroic acts. They have protected abandoned babies, alerted people in distress, and intervened in dangerous situations.

Hunger, abuse, and lack of care can lead to aggressive behavior in stray dogs. Humane approaches, such as feeding and medical aid, should be encouraged. Local authorities should promote stray dog welfare and crack down on illegal breeding.

The author concludes that stray dogs are not inherently malicious and their behavior is often the result of human actions. Rather than vilifying them, efforts should focus on finding solutions that ensure the safety of both humans and stray dogs.

### [Lessons from Ukraine: Time for India to crystallise military drone strategy](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/lessons-from-ukraine-time-for-india-to-crystallise-military-drone-strategy-13743470.html)

**Drones in Warfare and India's Strategy**

Recent conflicts, particularly in Ukraine, have highlighted the increasing significance of drones in warfare. In response, India is aiming to become a major drone producer and has taken steps to develop its industry.

**Global Drone Strategies:**

* The UK has announced a drone strategy investing $5.7 billion in unmanned systems.
* The Pentagon is shifting to employ thousands of expendable drones to counter China's advantages.
* China dominates the hobbyist drone market and is developing smaller drones for kamikaze strikes.

**Lessons from Ukraine:**

* Drones have been effective in targeting enemy assets, shaping outcomes, and creating psychological impact.
* Counterdrones have also evolved, such as electronic jamming.

**India's Drone Development:**

* India recognizes the future in drones and aims to develop a robust production sector.
* Indigenous drones, such as the "Tapas" and "Ghatak," are under development.
* Significant quantities of drones and swarms are being procured.

**Challenges and Considerations:**

* India faces challenges in indigenizing drone components and overcoming dependence on China.
* The military needs to develop doctrines and organizational structures to effectively utilize drones.
* India must strengthen its defenses against enemy drones and protect its territories from drone swarm attacks and terrorist threats.
* Joint training, operational synergy, and innovation are crucial for maximizing the potential of India's military drone ecosystem.

* Discusses the significance of diplomacy beyond mediation, highlighting the importance of strategic analysis and understanding adversaries.
* Emphasizes the need for India to define its interests, articulate positions, find solutions, and advance its model in the global arena.
* Documents the Modi government's efforts to improve India's international relations and engage with other nations.
* Examines the challenges and opportunities presented by global conflicts and uncertainties, such as the China-US rivalry, the Ukraine war, and supply chain disruptions.
* Presents a counterfactual analysis of Nehru's foreign policy, comparing it to the perspectives of Patel, Masani, and Rajagopalachari.

The book provides a contemporary perspective on India's position in the world, highlighting the importance of domestic preparedness and managing challenges such as transnational politics and extremism.

### [Pankaj Udhas’ passing: The end of an era](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/pankaj-udhas-passing-the-end-of-an-era-13742247.html)

Pankaj Udhas, a renowned ghazal singer, passed away, leaving a void in the world of Indian popular music. In the 1980s, Udhas emerged as a rising star, captivating audiences with his soulful renditions of ghazals and popular songs. He gained immense popularity and performed in concerts around the country. 

During that time, ghazals experienced a surge in popularity, with Mehdi Hassan, Ghulam Ali, and Jagjit Singh gaining widespread recognition. Udhas's debut album, "Aahat," was released in 1980 and featured hit songs like "Yeh Alag Baat Hai Saqi" and "Tum Aaye Zindagi Main." He had a unique ability to combine a beautiful voice with accessible lyrics, effortlessly connecting with the masses. 

Udhas's talent extended to film songs as well. He sang memorable tracks like "Chithhi Aayi Hai" from the movie "Naam," "Aaj Phir Tum Pe" from "Dayavan," and "Na Kajre Ki Dhaar" from "Mohra." 

Pankaj Udhas's journey through the world of music was marked by his dedication to ghazal singing. He leaves behind a legacy of timeless melodies that will continue to be cherished by his fans. His death marks the end of an era in Indian popular music.

### [Global Watch | China’s war on journalism: Largest prison for journalists in the world](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/global-watch-chinas-war-on-journalism-largest-prison-for-journalists-in-the-world-13600332.html)

China has been ranked as the largest prison for journalists in the world, with 121 journalists currently facing detention. Media freedom in China is classified as a "very serious situation" and is ranked 179th out of 180 countries. The Chinese Communist Party controls and funds most of the media, using it as a mouthpiece to spread propaganda. Journalists face censorship, harassment, and intimidation for reporting on sensitive information. The situation has worsened since Xi Jinping took leadership in 2012, with a culture of fear and restrictions on access to information. Hong Kong's press freedom has also declined since its handover to China in 1997, with media outlets coming under Chinese government control. The imposition of the National Security Law in 2020 further eroded media freedom and led to the closure of independent news outlets. Journalists have faced arrests, imprisonment, and attacks, resulting in a growing exodus of journalists from Hong Kong. Beijing's actions violate international norms and aim to control media narratives. The fate of journalism and democracy in Hong Kong is being closely watched.

### [Italy assumes G7 presidency amid global challenges: Focus on Ukraine, China and ethical AI](https://www.firstpost.com/opinion/italy-assumes-g7-presidency-amid-global-challenges-focus-on-ukraine-china-and-ethical-ai-13579862.html)

Italy has assumed the presidency of the G7, taking over from Japan. Italy's Prime Minister Giorgia Meloni will lead the group in addressing major global issues such as wars, climate change, Africa, and artificial intelligence. Italy's leadership comes at a critical time, with conflicts in Russia-Ukraine, Israel-Hamas, and challenges in the Red Sea and Bab-al-Mandab. Italy's exit from the Belt and Road Initiative and the need to "de-risk" economies from China are concerns. Additionally, Italy is dealing with an illegal immigration crisis and upcoming elections in the US and the European Union. The US emphasizes the importance of keeping Ukraine a priority. The G7 will focus on themes like China, food security, supply chain resilience, migration, energy security, and AI. Italy aims to prioritize the Global South, engage African nations, and invite the African Union, India, and the Ukrainian President to the G7 summit.
